<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Arlington National Cemetery</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg/220px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg/330px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg/440px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_National_Cemetery_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arlington National Cemetery and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Carillon" title="Netherlands Carillon">Netherlands Carillon</a> in December 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Arlington National Cemetery</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_national_cemetery" title="United States national cemetery">United States military cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arlington_County,_Virginia" title="Arlington County, Virginia">Arlington County, Virginia</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a> from <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, in whose 624 acres (253 ha) the dead of the nation's conflicts have been buried, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, as well as reinterred dead from earlier wars. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Army" title="United States Department of the Army">United States Department of the Army</a>, a component of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> (DOD), controls the cemetery.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_national_cemetery" title="United States national cemetery">national cemetery</a> was established during the Civil War on the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Arlington_House,_The_Robert_E._Lee_Memorial" title="Arlington House, The Robert E. Lee Memorial">Arlington House</a>, which had been the estate of the family of <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> general <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>'s wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Anna_Custis_Lee" title="Mary Anna Custis Lee">Mary Anna (Custis) Lee</a> (a great-granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Martha_Washington" title="Martha Washington">Martha Washington</a>). The Cemetery, along with Arlington House, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorial_Drive_(Arlington_National_Cemetery)" title="Memorial Drive (Arlington National Cemetery)">Memorial Drive</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Military_Service_for_America_Memorial" title="Women in Military Service for America Memorial">Hemicycle</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Memorial_Bridge" title="Arlington Memorial Bridge">Arlington Memorial Bridge</a>, form the <b>Arlington National Cemetery Historic District</b>, listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Like nearly all federal installations in Arlington County, it has a Washington, D.C. mailing address.</p>
<p>In recent years, the Department of the Army has made plans and taken actions to expand the cemetery onto public lands that the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> (NPS) controlled or that Arlington County or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Virginia" title="Commonwealth of Virginia">Commonwealth of Virginia</a> owned, as well as to restrict <a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">bicycle</a> use within the cemetery. These plans and actions have become controversial.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_(General_Lee%27s_home),_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn,_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2114" data-file-width="3000" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg/220px-East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg/330px-East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg/440px-East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_%28General_Lee%27s_home%29%2C_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn%2C_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East_front_of_Arlington_Mansion_(General_Lee%27s_home),_with_Union_soldiers_on_the_lawn,_06-28-1864_-_NARA_-_533118.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Custis Lee Mansion with Union soldiers on lawn</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Parke_Custis" title="George Washington Parke Custis">George Washington Parke Custis</a>, grandson of <a href="/wiki/Martha_Washington" title="Martha Washington">Martha Washington</a>, acquired the land that now is Arlington National Cemetery in 1802, and began construction of <a href="/wiki/Arlington_House,_The_Robert_E._Lee_Memorial" title="Arlington House, The Robert E. Lee Memorial">Arlington House</a> (the name is ultimately derived from the village of <a href="/wiki/Arlington,_Gloucestershire" title="Arlington, Gloucestershire">Arlington, Gloucestershire</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, where Custis' family was originally from). The estate passed to Custis' daughter, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Anna_Custis_Lee" title="Mary Anna Custis Lee">Mary Anna</a>, who had married <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> officer <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>. Custis' will gave a "life inheritance" to Mary Lee, allowing her to live at and run Arlington Estate for the rest of her life but not enabling her to sell any portion of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape62_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape62-4">[4]</a></sup> Upon her death, the Arlington estate passed to her eldest son, George Washington Custis Lee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape62_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape62-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Virginia seceded from the Union at the start of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Robert E. Lee resigned his commission on April 20, 1861, and took command of the armed forces of the Commonwealth of Virginia, later becoming commander of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Northern_Virginia" title="Army of Northern Virginia">Army of Northern Virginia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner-5">[5]</a></sup> On May 7, troops of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_militia" title="Virginia militia">Virginia militia</a> occupied Arlington and Arlington House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen69_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen69-6">[6]</a></sup> With Confederate forces occupying Arlington's high ground, the capital of the Union was left in an untenable military position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chase173_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chase173-7">[7]</a></sup> Although unwilling to leave Arlington House, Mary Lee believed her estate would soon be recaptured by federal soldiers. So she buried many of her family treasures on the grounds and left for her sister's estate at <a href="/wiki/Ravensworth_(plantation)" title="Ravensworth (plantation)">Ravensworth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fairfax_County,_Virginia" title="Fairfax County, Virginia">Fairfax County, Virginia</a>, on May 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atk25_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atk25-9">[9]</a></sup> On May 3, General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> ordered <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/Irvin_McDowell" title="Irvin McDowell">Irvin McDowell</a> to clear Arlington and the city of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria, Virginia</a>, of all troops not loyal to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> McDowell occupied Arlington without opposition on May 24.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Arlington_House.jpg/220px-Arlington_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Arlington_House.jpg/330px-Arlington_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Arlington_House.jpg/440px-Arlington_House.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Arlington_House,_The_Robert_E._Lee_Memorial" title="Arlington House, The Robert E. Lee Memorial">Arlington House</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the outbreak of the Civil War, most military personnel who died in battle near Washington, D.C., were buried at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Soldiers%27_and_Airmen%27s_Home_National_Cemetery" title="United States Soldiers' and Airmen's Home National Cemetery">United States Soldiers' Cemetery</a> in Washington, D.C., or <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_National_Cemetery_(Alexandria,_Virginia)" title="Alexandria National Cemetery (Alexandria, Virginia)">Alexandria Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria, Virginia</a>, but by late 1863 both were nearly full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape84_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape84-12">[12]</a></sup> On July 16, 1862, Congress passed legislation authorizing the U.S. federal government to purchase land for national cemeteries for military dead, and put the U.S. Army Quartermaster General in charge of this program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape84_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape84-12">[12]</a></sup> In May 1864, Union forces suffered large numbers of dead in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Wilderness" title="Battle of the Wilderness">Battle of the Wilderness</a>. <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Quartermaster General of the United States Army">Quartermaster General</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montgomery_C._Meigs_(1816%E2%80%931892)" title="Montgomery C. Meigs (1816–1892)">Montgomery C. Meigs</a> ordered that an examination of eligible sites be made for the establishment for a large new national military cemetery. Within weeks, his staff reported that Arlington Estate was the most suitable property in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape84_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape84-12">[12]</a></sup> The property was high and free from floods (which might unearth graves), it had a view of the District of Columbia, and it was aesthetically pleasing. It was also the home of the leader of the armed forces of the Confederate States of America, and denying Robert E. Lee use of his home after the war was a valuable political consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape88_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape88-13">[13]</a></sup> The first military burial at Arlington, for <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Christman" title="William Henry Christman">William Henry Christman</a>, was made on May 13, 1864,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape86_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape86-14">[14]</a></sup> close to what is now the northeast gate in Section 27.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennee4_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennee4-15">[15]</a></sup> However, Meigs did not formally authorize establishment of burials until June 15, 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landscape85_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landscape85-16">[16]</a></sup> Arlington did not desegregate its burial practices until President Harry S. Truman issued Executive Order 9981 on July 26, 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1818" data-file-width="3479" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg/220px-SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg/330px-SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg/440px-SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SMA_Dunway_Burial_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3rd_US_Infantry_Regiment_(The_Old_Guard)" title="3rd US Infantry Regiment (The Old Guard)">The Old Guard</a> transports the flag-draped casket of the second <a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Major_of_the_Army" title="Sergeant Major of the Army">Sergeant Major of the Army</a> <a href="/wiki/George_W._Dunaway" title="George W. Dunaway">George W. Dunaway</a> who was buried with full military honors at Arlington National Cemetery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The government acquired Arlington at a tax sale in 1864 for $26,800, equal to $410,000 today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughes_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-hughes-18">[18]</a></sup> Mrs. Lee had not appeared in person but rather had sent an agent, attempting to pay the $92.07 in property taxes (equal to $1,400 today) assessed on the estate in a timely manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arlingtoncemetery.org_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-arlingtoncemetery.org-19">[19]</a></sup> The government turned away her agent, refusing to accept the tendered payment. In 1874, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Custis_Lee" title="Custis Lee">Custis Lee</a>, heir under his grandfather's will passing the estate in trust to his mother, sued the United States claiming ownership of Arlington. On December 9, 1882, the U.S. Supreme Court ruled 5–4 in Lee's favor in <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Lee_(1882)" title="United States v. Lee (1882)">United States v. Lee</a></i>, deciding that Arlington had been confiscated without due process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arlingtoncemetery.org_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-arlingtoncemetery.org-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> After that decision, Congress returned the estate to him, and on March 3, 1883, Custis Lee sold it back to the government for $150,000 (equal to $3,271,364 in 2017) at a signing ceremony with Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln" title="Robert Todd Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughes_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-hughes-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explore_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-explore-21">[21]</a></sup> The land then became a military reservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schildt_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schildt-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> conducted the first national Memorial Day ceremony in Arlington National Cemetery, on May 30, 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presidency_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-presidency-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Freedmen's_Village"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedmen.27s_Village">Freedmen's Village</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Freedmen's Village">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beginning in 1863, the federal government used the southern portion of the land now occupied by the cemetery as a settlement for freed slaves, giving the name of "Freedmen's Village" to the land. The government constructed rental houses that 1,100 to 3,000 freed slaves eventually occupied while farming 1,100 acres (450 ha) of the estate and receiving schooling and occupational training during the Civil War and after War ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explore_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-explore-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> However, after the land became part of a military reservation, the government asked the Villagers to leave. When some remained, John A. Commerford, the Superintendent of Arlington National Cemetery, asked the <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Quartermaster General of the United States Army">Army's Quartermaster General</a> in 1887 to close the Village on the grounds that people living in the Village had been taking trees at night from the cemetery for use as firewood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schildt_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schildt-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The Quartermaster General and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> then approved Commerford's request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schildt_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schildt-22">[22]</a></sup> The last of the Village's residents departed after the <a href="/wiki/56th_United_States_Congress" title="56th United States Congress">56th United States Congress</a> appropriated $75,000 in 1900 to settle the government's debts to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schildt_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schildt-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_expansion">Recent expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recent expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With limited space but large numbers of World War II, Korean War, Vietnam War, and other veterans dying and wanting to be buried at Arlington, the need for additional burial space at the cemetery became a pressing issue. In 1991, Cemetery superintendent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_C._Metzler,_Jr." title="John C. Metzler, Jr.">John C. Metzler, Jr.</a>, implemented a $1.4 million plan to clear a former 13 acres (5.3 ha) parking lot to create space for about 9,000 new grave sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaplowExpanded_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-KaplowExpanded-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Cemetery received the authority to transfer 12 acres (4.9 ha) of woodland from the NPS-controlled <a href="/wiki/Arlington_House,_The_Robert_E._Lee_Memorial" title="Arlington House, The Robert E. Lee Memorial">Arlington House</a> in 1996<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-law-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wee_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wee-28">[28]</a></sup> and 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law2_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-law2-30">[30]</a></sup> 37 acres (15 ha) of land in 1999 from the DOD that was the site of the <a href="/wiki/Navy_Annex" title="Navy Annex">Navy Annex</a> building,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vogel_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law3_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-law3-32">[32]</a></sup> 8 acres (3.2 ha) of land in 1999 from the Department of the Army that was part of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myer" title="Fort Myer">Fort Myer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vogel_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law5_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-law5-33">[33]</a></sup> 4 acres (1.6 ha) of land from Arlington County's Southgate Road right-of-way in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law4_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-law4-34">[34]</a></sup> and just under 10 acres (4.0 ha) of land from Fort Myer in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuaneWarriors_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuaneWarriors-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3029" data-file-width="2412" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG/220px-Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG/330px-Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG/440px-Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graves_at_Arlington_on_Memorial_Day.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Headstone" title="Headstone">Gravestones</a> at Arlington National Cemetery are marked by U.S. flags each <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Day" title="Memorial Day">Memorial Day</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2007, Metzler implemented the Millennium Project, a $35 million expansion plan to begin utilizing the Arlington woodland, Fort Myer, and Navy Annex land. The project also included converting 40 acres (16 ha) of unused space and 4 acres (16,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of maintenance property on the cemetery grounds into burial space in 2006 and 2007 to allow an additional 26,000 graves and 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Cremation#Methods_of_keeping_or_disposing_of_the_cremated_remains" title="Cremation">inurnments</a>. The Millennium Project expanded the cemetery's physical boundaries for the first time since the 1960s, and was the largest expansion of burial space at the site since the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuaneWarriors_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-RuaneWarriors-35">[35]</a></sup> Several environmental and historical preservation groups criticized Metzler's plans, as did the NPS and the manager of Arlington House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuaneWarriors_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-RuaneWarriors-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gearan_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gearan-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 26, 2013, the Consolidated and Further Continuing Appropriations Act, 2013 (<a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Public Law</a> 113-6) appropriated to the DOD $84 million to plan, design and construct the Millennium Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-appropriation1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-appropriation1-38">[38]</a></sup> The legislation additionally appropriated to the DOD $19 million to study, plan and design a future expansion of the cemetery's burial space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-appropriation1_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-appropriation1-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy_Annex_property_expansion_controversy">Navy Annex property expansion controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Navy Annex property expansion controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1998, a Congressional proposal to expand the cemetery onto land that the Navy Annex and Fort Myer then occupied led to concerns that Arlington County officials had not been properly consulted, leading to the withdrawal of the proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Authorization_Act" title="National Defense Authorization Act">National Defense Authorization Act</a> for Fiscal Year 2000 (Public Law 106-65), which was enacted into law during October 1999, subsequently required the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a> to transfer to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">Secretary of the Army</a> administrative jurisdiction of the 36 acres (15 ha) Navy Annex property. The Act required the Secretary of Defense to demolish the Annex's buildings and prepare the property for use as part of the cemetery, while requiring the Secretary of the Army to incorporate the Annex property into the cemetery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law3_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-law3-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2013, the County Manager of Arlington County, Virginia, and the Executive Director of the Army National Military Cemeteries (consisting of Arlington National Cemetery and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Soldiers%27_and_Airmen%27s_Home_National_Cemetery" title="United States Soldiers' and Airmen's Home National Cemetery">United States Soldiers' and Airmen's Home National Cemetery</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> signed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorandum_of_Understanding" title="Memorandum of Understanding">Memorandum of Understanding</a> (MOU) between the <a href="/wiki/Arlington_County,_Virginia#Government_and_politics" title="Arlington County, Virginia">Arlington County Board</a> and the Department of the Army to expand the cemetery even further. Under the tentative plan, Arlington County would give up the <a href="/wiki/Easement" title="Easement">easement</a> for Southgate Road (which lies between the Navy Annex property and the cemetery's 2012 boundary), and obtain a narrow easement along the southwest border of the Navy Annex site for a new Southgate Road. In exchange, the Department of Defense would give the Navy Annex parking lot to the county.</p>
<p>The Army would also transfer land west of South Joyce Street to <a href="/wiki/Virginia_State_Route_244" title="Virginia State Route 244">Columbia Pike</a> to Arlington County. Additionally, the Commonwealth of Virginia would convey to the cemetery roughly the northern half of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Department_of_Transportation" title="Virginia Department of Transportation">Virginia Department of Transportation</a> land bounded by South Joyce Street, Columbia Pike, and South <a href="/wiki/Washington_Boulevard_(Arlington)" title="Washington Boulevard (Arlington)">Washington Boulevard</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cloverleaf_interchange" title="Cloverleaf interchange">cloverleaf interchange</a> between Columbia Pike and S. Washington Blvd. would be eliminated, and the <a href="/wiki/Hairpin_turn" title="Hairpin turn">hairpin turn</a> in Columbia Pike straightened, to provide a safer, more natural exit from S. Washington Blvd. onto Columbia Pike. Although exact acreages were not specified and the plan depended upon the Commonwealth of Virginia's cooperation, the MOU if implemented would have created a more contiguous plot of land for the cemetery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, in December 2016, the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_Authorization_Act_for_Fiscal_Year_2017" title="National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2017">National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2017</a> (Public Law 114-328) authorized the Secretary of the Army to expand the cemetery by acquiring from Arlington County and the Commonwealth of Virginia by condemnation and other means properties near the cemetery that contain the Southgate Road, South Joyce Street and Washington Boulevard right-of-ways, including the Washington Boulvard-Columbia Pike interchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Army then informed the Arlington County government in June 2017 that the Army would no longer pursue a land exchange with the County. The Army told the County that the Army would use the entire Navy Annex site to expand the cemetery and would acquire for the cemetery about 5 acres (2.0 ha) of public land that Arlington County then owned. The Army would also acquire for the cemetery expansion about 7 acres (2.8 ha) of land located between Columbia Pike and <a href="/wiki/Interstate_395_(Virginia%E2%80%93District_of_Columbia)" title="Interstate 395 (Virginia–District of Columbia)">Interstate 395</a> that the Commonwealth of Virginia then owned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arlington_Woods_expansion_controversy">Arlington Woods expansion controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Arlington Woods expansion controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1325" data-file-width="2000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg/220px-Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg/330px-Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg/440px-Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_NW_at_Arlington_Woods_-_Section_29_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery_-_2013-01-18.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A portion of Arlington Woods on Humphreys Drive (2013)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On February 22, 1995, officials of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a> and the United States Department of the Army signed an agreement to transfer from Arlington House, The Robert E. Lee Memorial, to the Army a part of Arlington Woods, which was located in Section 29 of the NPS at Arlington National Cemetery between Arlington House and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myer" title="Fort Myer">Fort Myer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The property transfer, which involved 12 acres (4.9 ha) of NPS land, was intended to permit Metzler to start expanding the cemetery beyond its existing boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wee_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wee-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanna_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanna-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Environmentalists expressed concerns that the agreement would result in the partial destruction of the 24 acres (9.7 ha) remnant of an historically important stand of native trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gearan_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gearan-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> A historical marker near the woodland notes that, while visiting Arlington House in 1825, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette</a> had warned <a href="/wiki/Mary_Lee_Fitzhugh_Custis" title="Mary Lee Fitzhugh Custis">Mary Lee Fitzhugh Custis</a>, the wife of George Washington Parke Custis: "Cherish these forest trees around your mansion. Recollect how much easier it is to cut a tree than to make one grow." The marker further notes that the Virginia Native Plant Society had recognized the woodland as being one of the best examples of old growth terraced gravel forest remaining in Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 23, 1996, the National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 1997 (Public Law 104-201) authorized the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> to transfer to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">Secretary of the Army</a> all of the land in Section 29 that was within an "Arlington National Cemetery Interment Zone" and some of the land in the Section that was within a "Robert E. Lee Memorial Preservation Zone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-law-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wee_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wee-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 5, 1998, the NPS, which is a component of the Department of the Interior, informed the <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Planning_Commission" title="National Capital Planning Commission">National Capital Planning Commission</a> that it wanted to transfer only 4 acres (1.6 ha) to the cemetery, rather than the 12 acres (4.9 ha) that the 1995 agreement had described. In response, Metzler stated: "I was surprised. But we will continue to work with the Department of Interior and see what happens."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wee_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wee-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 12, 1999, the NPS issued a <a href="/wiki/Federal_Register" title="Federal Register">Federal Register</a> notice that announced the availability of an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_assessment#United_States" title="Environmental impact assessment">environmental assessment</a> (EA) for the transfer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanna_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hanna-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup> The EA stated that the Interment Zone contained the oldest and largest tract of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climax_vegetation" title="Climax vegetation">climax</a> <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_mixed_forests" title="Southeastern mixed forests">eastern hardwood forest</a> in Arlington County. This forest was the same type that once covered the Arlington estate, and had regenerated from trees that were present historically. A forestry study determined that a representative tree was 258 years old. The Interment Zone was also determined to contain significant archeological and cultural landscape resources, in addition to those in the Preservation Zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup> The EA described four alternative courses of action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In contrast to the NPS's March 1998 statement to the National Capital Planning Commission, the 1999 EA stated that the preferred alternative (Alternative 1) would transfer to the cemetery approximately 9.6 acres (3.9 ha), comprising most of the Interment Zone and the northern tip of the Preservation Zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup> Another alternative (Alternative 3) would transfer to the cemetery the 12 acres (4.9 ha) Interment Zone, while keeping the 12.5 acres (5.1 ha) Preservation Zone under NPS jurisdiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup> The EA concluded: "Public Law 104-201 directed the Secretary of the Interior to transfer to the Secretary of the Army jurisdiction over the Interment Zone, which is the plan in Alternative 3. Adoption of any of the other alternatives would require legislative action to amend the existing law."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assessment1_48-5"><a href="#cite_note-assessment1-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1174" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg/350px-Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg/525px-Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg/700px-Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Millennium_Project_-_Arlington_National_Cemetery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing the Millennium Project's expansion of Arlington National Cemetery into Arlington Woods and Fort Myer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 28, 2001, the National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2002 (Public Law 107-107) repealed the "obsolete" part of Public Law 104-201 that had authorized the transfer of portions of Section 29 to the Secretary of the Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law2_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-law2-30">[30]</a></sup> The new legislation required the Secretary of the Interior to transfer to the Secretary of the Army within 30 days the approximately 12 acres (4.9 ha) Interment Zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law2_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-law2-30">[30]</a></sup> The transfer therefore involved the entire 12 acres (4.9 ha) of NPS land that the 1995 agreement and Alternative 3 in the 1999 EA had described.</p>
<p>The 2001 legislation required the Secretary of the Army to use the Interment Zone for in-ground burial sites and <a href="/wiki/Columbarium" title="Columbarium">columbarium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law2_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-law2-30">[30]</a></sup> In addition, the legislation required the Secretary of the Interior to manage the remainder of Section 29 "in perpetuity to provide a natural setting and visual buffer for Arlington House, the Robert E. Lee Memorial."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law2_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-law2-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 12, 2012, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">United States Army Corps of Engineers</a> asked for comments on a draft EA that described a further expansion of Arlington National Cemetery as part of the Millennium Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA2-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The 2012 draft EA was intended to implement conversion into burial space of the 17 acres (6.9 ha) of Ft. Myer grounds as well as 10 acres (4.0 ha) of Section 29 woodland. The draft EA described seven alternatives. The preferred alternative (Alternative E) called for the removal of about one-half of the 1,700 trees with a diameter of 6 inches (15 cm) or greater on the site. About 640 of the trees were within a 135-year-old portion of Arlington Woods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA3_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA3-52">[52]</a></sup> The draft EA concluded: "Based on the evaluation of environmental impacts....., no significant impacts would be expected from the Proposed Action; therefore, an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_statement" title="Environmental impact statement">Environmental Impact Statement</a> will not be prepared and a <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_statement#NEPA_process" title="Environmental impact statement">Finding of No Significant Impact</a> will be prepared and signed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA3_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-EA3-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 12, 2013, the Corps of Engineers released a revised EA for the Millennium Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corps_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-corps-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The revised EA contained copies of a number of public comments on the draft EA that had criticized the project and parts of the EA while proposing alternative locations for new military burials near the cemetery and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> However, the Department of Forestry of the Commonwealth of Virginia found that, based on information in the draft EA, the project would not have a significant adverse impact on the Commonwealth's forest resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The revised EA did not change the preferred alternative (Alternative E) or the Army's plans to prepare and sign the Finding of No Significant Impact (FONSI) that the draft EA had described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 5, 2013, after reviewing 100 public comments that it had received on the revised EA, the Corps of Engineers released a final EA and a signed FONSI for the Millennium Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalEA_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-finalEA-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The Final EA and the FONSI retained Alternative E as the preferred alternative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalEA_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-finalEA-58">[58]</a></sup> The final EA stated that, of the 905 trees to be removed, 771 trees were healthy native trees that had diameters between 6 and 41 inches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p114_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-p114-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The project would remove approximately 211 trees from a less than 2.63 acres (1.06 ha) area containing a portion of a 145-year-old forest that stood within the property boundaries of a historic district that a <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> nomination form for Arlington House had described in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p114_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-p114-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> About 491 trees would be removed from an area of trees that was approximately 105 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p114_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-p114-60">[60]</a></sup> Approximately 203 trees with ages of 50 to 145 years would be removed from a former picnic area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p114_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-p114-60">[60]</a></sup> At a public hearing on July 11, 2013, the National Capital Planning Commission approved the site and building plans for the Millennium Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_mismanagement_controversy">2010 mismanagement controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2010 mismanagement controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery_mismanagement_controversy" title="Arlington National Cemetery mismanagement controversy">Arlington National Cemetery mismanagement controversy</a></div>
<p>On June 9, 2010, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">United States Secretary of the Army</a> <a href="/wiki/John_M._McHugh" title="John M. McHugh">John M. McHugh</a> reprimanded the cemetery's superintendent, John C. Metzler, Jr., and his deputy, Thurman Higgenbotham, after a <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Inspector_General,_U.S._Department_of_Defense" title="Office of the Inspector General, U.S. Department of Defense">DOD inspector general</a>'s report revealed that cemetery officials had placed the wrong headstones on tombs, buried coffins in shallow graves, and buried bodies on top of one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes2010_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2010-64">[64]</a></sup> Metzler, who had already announced his intention to retire on July 2, 2010, admitted some mistakes had been made but denied allegations of widespread or serious mismanagement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes2010_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes2010-64">[64]</a></sup> The investigation also found that cemetery employees were burdened in their day-to-day work by "dysfunctional management, lack of established policy and procedures, and an overall unhealthy organizational climate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McMichael_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-McMichael-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-66">[66]</a></sup> Both Metzler and Higgenbotham retired soon after the investigation commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis.2C_Aaron_C._2010_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis.2C_Aaron_C._2010-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2011, as a result of the problems discovered, Kathryn Condon, the recently appointed Executive Director of the Army National Military Cemeteries, announced that the cemetery's staff had been increased from 102 to 159. She added that the cemetery was also acquiring additional equipment because, "They didn't have the proper equipment to do the job really to the standard they needed to do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mismanagement controversy included a limitation on mass media access to funerals, which also proved controversial. Until 2005, the cemetery's administration gave free access, with the family's permission, to the press to cover funerals at the cemetery. According to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> in 2008, the cemetery gradually imposed increasing restrictions on media coverage of funerals beginning about 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Management_turnover">Management turnover</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Management turnover">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the cemetery's management controversy began to end, the Army appointed Patrick K. Hallinan the acting superintendent of the cemetery in June 2010. He was promoted permanently to the position in October 2010. Hallinan had previously worked for the Office of Field Programs in the National Cemetery Administration, an agency of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans%27_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans' Affairs">United States Department of Veterans' Affairs</a>. In that capacity, Hallinan had oversight of 131 national cemeteries, national cemetery policy, procedures, and operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Hallinan was promoted to Executive Director of the Army National Military Cemeteries upon the retirement of Kathryn Condon in spring 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lechnerappointed_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-lechnerappointed-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2014, Hallinan stepped down and was replaced by Jack E. Lechner, Jr. as superintendent of the cemetery. Lechner had been a <a href="/wiki/Funeral_director" title="Funeral director">funeral director</a> for 10 years in the private sector before joining the U.S. Army. He rose to the rank of colonel (retiring in November 2011), having spent 2008 to 2010 as chief of the Supply Division of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, overseeing the equipping of Iraqi and Afghanistan national security forces. Since June 2010, he had served as executive officer and deputy superintendent of the cemetery under Hallinan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lechnerappointed_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-lechnerappointed-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Army removed Lechner as superintendent of the cemetery in early August 2015 after a performance review "called into question his ability to serve successfully as a senior leader". The Army declined to elaborate further and appointed Hallinan to be the temporary Cemetery superintendent until the Army could find a successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wreaths_Across_America">Wreaths Across America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wreaths Across America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg/220px-Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg/330px-Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg/440px-Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wreaths_Across_America_131214-A-SW162-006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wreaths laid at Arlington National Cemetery, December 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning in 1992, the Morrill Worcester wreath company in <a href="/wiki/Harrington,_Maine" title="Harrington, Maine">Harrington, Maine</a>, had a surplus at the end of the holiday season. Recalling a boyhood trip to the cemetery, Worcester donated to the cemetery 5,000 wreaths to honor the cemetery's dead, with the help of volunteers and a local trucking company. After thirteen years of similar donations, in 2005 a photo of snowy gravestones covered with wreaths at the cemetery received widespread circulation on the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>. Thousands of people called Worcester, wanting to replicate the wreath-laying service at their own veteran cemeteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wreaths_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wreaths-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, over 150 different locations simultaneously held ceremonies with Arlington's. Additionally, the project had its first “Veterans Honor Parade” with "Patriot Guard Riders" who escorted the wreaths from Maine to the cemetery. The parade, which is held each year, now visits schools, monuments, veterans’ homes and communities along its route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wreaths_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wreaths-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of 2006, Worcester's company supplied wreaths to over 230 state and national cemeteries and veterans monuments across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Assisted by veterans and truckers, Worcester established in 2007 a nonprofit organization named "Wreaths Across America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wreaths_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wreaths-73">[73]</a></sup> In December 2008, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> agreed to a resolution that designated December 13, 2008, as “Wreaths Across America Day”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, volunteers placed over 700,000 memorial wreaths at 1,000 locations in the United States and overseas, including the <a href="/wiki/USS_Arizona_Memorial" title="USS Arizona Memorial">USS Arizona Memorial</a> at <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill" title="Battle of Bunker Hill">Bunker Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_September_11_Memorial_%26_Museum" title="National September 11 Memorial &amp; Museum">National September 11 Memorial</a> at the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_site" title="World Trade Center site">World Trade Center site</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. During that year, volunteers were able to place wreaths in all sections of the cemetery for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wreaths_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wreaths-73">[73]</a></sup> In 2016, this number increased to 1.2 million wreaths being placed at more than 1,230 cemeteries across the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conflict of interest charges have been made against Wreaths Across America because this charity has an exclusive for-profit supplier, Worcestor Wreath Company, also run by the Morrill family in the same town. The charity's purchases of wreaths from this company account for most of the company's revenue and profits. In late 2015, the Wall Street Journal reported serious conflicts of interest with potential malfeasance in governance and contracting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 2015 alone the WSJ reported profits of over $1 million on sales of over 850,000 wreaths to the charity raising concerns about competitive bidding, reporting that several competitors had asked to bid significantly below the price offered by Worcestor Wreath Company but were denied access.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="150th_anniversary">150th anniversary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 150th anniversary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During May and June 2014, the cemetery celebrated the 150th anniversary of its founding with a month-long series of events, tours, and lectures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> During these celebrations, cemetery officials formally re-designated the Old Amphitheater as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Tanner_Amphitheater" title="James Tanner Amphitheater">James Tanner Amphitheater</a>. <a href="/wiki/James_R._Tanner" title="James R. Tanner">James R. Tanner</a> was a Union Army officer who lost both legs during the war. He later became a War Department stenographer, and recorded much of the early evidence in the investigation into the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">assassination of Abraham Lincoln</a>. He later was active in the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a>, a Union Army veterans group. Tanner is buried a few yards from the amphitheater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sections">Sections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3414" data-file-width="5141" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg/200px-USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg/300px-USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg/400px-USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA-Arlington_National_Cemetery0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arlington National Cemetery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Cemetery is divided into 70 sections, with some sections in the southeast and western part of the cemetery reserved for future expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Section 60, in the southeast part of the cemetery, is the burial ground for military personnel killed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_War_on_Terror" title="Global War on Terror">Global War on Terror</a> since 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Section 21, also known as the Nurses Section, is the area of Arlington National Cemetery where many nurses are buried and is the site of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish-American_War_Nurses_Memorial" title="Spanish-American War Nurses Memorial">Spanish-American War Nurses Memorial</a> and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nurses_Memorial_(Arlington_National_Cemetery)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nurses Memorial (Arlington National Cemetery) (page does not exist)">Nurses Memorial</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Another section—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaplains_Hill" title="Chaplains Hill">Chaplains Hill</a>—includes monuments to Jewish, Protestant, and Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Military_chaplain" title="Military chaplain">military chaplains</a>. In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> soldiers buried at the Soldiers' Home and various locations within Arlington were reinterred in a Confederate section that was authorized by Congress in 1900. On June 4, 1914, the <a href="/wiki/United_Daughters_of_the_Confederacy" title="United Daughters of the Confederacy">United Daughters of the Confederacy</a> dedicated the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Memorial_(Arlington_National_Cemetery)" title="Confederate Memorial (Arlington National Cemetery)">Confederate Memorial</a> designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moses_Ezekiel" title="Moses Ezekiel">Moses Ezekiel</a>. Upon his death in 1917, Ezekiel was buried at the base of the monument as he was a veteran of the Confederate army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> All Confederate headstones in this section are peaked rather than rounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-markers_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-markers-84">[84]</a></sup> More than 3,800 former slaves, called "Contrabands" during the Civil War, are buried in Section 27. Their headstones are designated with the word "Civilian" or "Citizen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facts_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-facts-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Grave_markers,_niches,_and_headstones"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Grave_markers.2C_niches.2C_and_headstones">Grave markers, niches, and headstones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Grave markers, niches, and headstones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">United States Department of Veterans Affairs</a> oversees the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Cemetery_Administration" title="National Cemetery Administration">National Cemetery Administration</a>'s orders<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orders_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-orders-86">[86]</a></sup> for placement of inscriptions and faith emblems at no charge to the estate of the deceased, submitted with information provided by the next of kin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VA40_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-VA40-87">[87]</a></sup> that is placed on upright marble headstones or columbarium niche covers. The Department of Veterans Affairs currently offers 63 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs_emblems_for_headstones_and_markers" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs emblems for headstones and markers">authorized faith emblems</a> for placement on markers to represent the deceased's faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VA_Emblem_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-VA_Emblem-88">[88]</a></sup> This number has grown in recent years due to legal challenges to policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACLU_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACLU-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to 2007, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">United States Department of Veterans Affairs</a> (VA) did not allow the use of the <a href="/wiki/Pentacle" title="Pentacle">pentacle</a> as an "emblem of belief" on tombstones in military cemeteries. This policy was changed following an out-of-court settlement on April 23 following a series of lawsuits by the family of <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Stewart_(soldier)" title="Patrick Stewart (soldier)">Patrick Stewart</a> against the VA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SymbolsAllowed_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-SymbolsAllowed-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VAMustAccommodate_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-VAMustAccommodate-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ListofVASymbols_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ListofVASymbols-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1947 and 2001, privately purchased markers were permitted in the cemetery. The sections in which the cemetery permitted such markers are nearly filled and the cemetery generally does not allow new burials in these sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burial_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-burial-93">[93]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the older sections of the cemetery have a wide variety of private markers placed prior to 2001, including an artillery piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 32 <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth" title="Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> war dead burials and some headstones are <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_War_Graves_Commission" title="Commonwealth War Graves Commission">Commonwealth War Graves Commission</a> style.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arlington_Memorial_Amphitheater">Arlington Memorial Amphitheater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Arlington Memorial Amphitheater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Memorial_Amphitheater" title="Arlington Memorial Amphitheater">Arlington Memorial Amphitheater</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="1002" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg/200px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg/300px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg/400px-Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_National_Cemetery_Amphitheater_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of Memorial Amphitheater</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Tomb of the Unknowns is part of the <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Memorial_Amphitheater" title="Arlington Memorial Amphitheater">Arlington Memorial Amphitheater</a>. The Memorial Amphitheater has hosted state funerals and <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Day" title="Memorial Day">Memorial Day</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterans_Day_(United_States)" title="Veterans Day (United States)">Veterans Day</a> ceremonies. Ceremonies are also held for <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>. About 5,000 people attend these holiday ceremonies each year. The structure is mostly built of Imperial Danby marble from <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. The Memorial Display room, between the amphitheater and the Tomb of the Unknowns, uses <a href="/wiki/Botticino" title="Botticino">Botticino</a> stone, imported from <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. The amphitheater was the result of a campaign by <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Kimball" title="Ivory Kimball">Ivory Kimball</a> to construct a place to honor America's servicemen/women. Congress authorized the structure on March 4, 1913. <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> laid the cornerstone for the building on October 15, 1915. The cornerstone contained 15 items including a Bible and a copy of the Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amphitheatre_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-amphitheatre-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the Arlington Memorial Amphitheater was completed in 1921, important ceremonies were held at what is now known as the "Old Amphitheater." This structure sits where Robert E. Lee once had his gardens. The amphitheater was built in 1868 under the direction of Civil War General <a href="/wiki/John_A._Logan" title="John A. Logan">John A. Logan</a>. Gen. <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> was the featured speaker at the <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Day" title="Memorial Day">Decoration Day</a> dedication ceremony, May 30, 1868, later being elected as President of the United States 1881. The amphitheater has an encircling colonnade with a latticed roof that once supported a web of vines. The amphitheater has a marble <a href="/wiki/Dais" title="Dais">dais</a>, known as "the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rostrum" title="wiktionary:rostrum">rostrum</a>", which is inscribed with the U.S. national motto found on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_United_States" title="Great Seal of the United States">Great Seal of the United States</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/E_pluribus_unum" title="E pluribus unum">E pluribus unum</a></i> ("Out of many, one"). The rostrum was designed by General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montgomery_C._Meigs_(1816%E2%80%931892)" title="Montgomery C. Meigs (1816–1892)">Montgomery C. Meigs</a>, then Quartermaster General of the U.S. Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poole-96">[96]</a></sup> The amphitheater seats 1,500 people and has hosted speakers such as <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-old_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-old-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_memorials_and_monuments_at_Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="List of memorials and monuments at Arlington National Cemetery">List of memorials and monuments at Arlington National Cemetery</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomb_of_the_Unknowns">Tomb of the Unknowns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tomb of the Unknowns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Unknowns" title="Tomb of the Unknowns">Tomb of the Unknowns</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2388" data-file-width="3712" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg/220px-Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg/330px-Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg/440px-Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldiers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Tomb of the Unknowns with guards</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Unknowns" title="Tomb of the Unknowns">Tomb of the Unknown Soldier</a> stands on top of a hill overlooking Washington, D.C. One of the more well-attended sites at the cemetery, the tomb is made from Yule marble quarried in Colorado. It consists of seven pieces, with a total weight of 79 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a> (72 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a>). The tomb was completed and opened to the public April 9, 1932, at a cost of $48,000.</p>
<p>It was initially named the "Tomb of the Unknown Soldier." Other unknown servicemen were later entombed there, and it became known as the "Tomb of the Unknowns", though it has never been officially named. The soldiers entombed there are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Unknown Soldier of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, interred November 11, 1921. President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> presided.</li>
<li>Unknown Soldier of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, interred May 30, 1958. President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> presided.</li>
<li>Unknown Soldier of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, also interred May 30, 1958. President Dwight Eisenhower presided again, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> acted as next of kin.</li>
<li>Unknown Soldier of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, interred May 28, 1984. President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> presided. The remains of the Vietnam Unknown were disinterred, under the authority of President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, on May 14, 1998, and were identified as those of Air Force 1st Lt. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Blassie" title="Michael Blassie">Michael J. Blassie</a>, whose family had them reinterred near their home in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>. It has been determined that the crypt at the Tomb of the Unknowns that contained the remains of the Vietnam Unknown will remain empty.
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The Tomb of the Unknowns has been perpetually guarded since July 2, 1937, by the U.S. Army. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3rd_United_States_Infantry_Regiment_(TOG)" title="3rd United States Infantry Regiment (TOG)">3rd U.S. Infantry Regiment</a> ("The Old Guard") began guarding the Tomb on April 6, 1948. There is a meticulous routine which the guard follows when watching over the graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The Tomb Guard:</p>
<ol>
<li>Marches 21 steps down the black mat behind the Tomb.</li>
<li>Turns, faces east for 21 seconds.</li>
<li>Turns and faces north for 21 seconds.</li>
<li>Takes 21 steps down the mat.</li>
<li>Repeats the routine until the soldier is relieved of duty at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guard_Mounting" title="Guard Mounting">Changing of the Guard</a>.</li>
</ol>
<p>After each turn, the Guard executes a sharp "shoulder-arms" movement to place the weapon on the shoulder closest to the visitors to signify that the Guard stands between the Tomb and any possible threat.</p>
<p>Twenty-one was chosen because it symbolizes the highest military honor that can be bestowed—the 21-gun salute.</p>
<p>Each turn the guard makes precise movements and followed by a loud click of the heels as he snaps them together. The guard is changed every half-hour during daylight in the summer, and every hour during daylight in the winter and every two hours at night (when the cemetery is closed to the public), regardless of weather conditions.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_Amphitheater_1922_U.S._stamp.1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="238" data-file-width="272" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Arlington_Amphitheater_1922_U.S._stamp.1.jpg/220px-Arlington_Amphitheater_1922_U.S._stamp.1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/33/Arlington_Amphitheater_1922_U.S._stamp.1.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arlington_Amphitheater_1922_U.S._stamp.1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Arlington Amphitheater<br/>
1922 issue</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A commemorative stamp was issued on November 11, 1922, the first anniversary of the first entombment picturing the Amphitheater. It encompasses the original Tomb of the Unknown Soldier. The remains of an unidentified WW I American soldier was entombed on Armistice Day, November 11, 1921, later covered in 1931 by a more elaborate marble sarcophagus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_memorials">Other memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arlington_National_Cemetery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Maine_Mast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1200" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/USS_Maine_Mast.jpg/200px-USS_Maine_Mast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/USS_Maine_Mast.jpg/300px-USS_Maine_Mast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/USS_Maine_Mast.jpg/400px-USS_Maine_Mast.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Maine_Mast.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>Maine</i> Mast Memorial</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are several memorials on the grounds of the cemetery. However, due to the lack of space for burials and the large amount of space that memorials take up, the U.S. Army now requires a joint or concurrent resolution from Congress before it will place new memorials at Arlington.</p>
<p>Near the Tomb of the Unknowns stands the <a href="/wiki/USS_Maine_Mast_Memorial" title="USS Maine Mast Memorial"><i>USS Maine</i> Mast Memorial</a>, which commemorates the 266 men who lost their lives aboard the <a href="/wiki/USS_Maine_(ACR-1)" title="USS Maine (ACR-1)">USS <i>Maine</i></a>.
